<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Lift (force)</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Lift_(disambiguation)" title="Lift (disambiguation)">Lift (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wright_Glider_1902.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="447" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Wright_Glider_1902.jpg/220px-Wright_Glider_1902.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Wright_Glider_1902.jpg/330px-Wright_Glider_1902.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Wright_Glider_1902.jpg/440px-Wright_Glider_1902.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wright_Glider_1902.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1902 <a href="/wiki/Wright_Glider" title="Wright Glider">Wright Glider</a> shows its lift by pulling up</div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluid</a> flowing past the surface of a body exerts a <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a> on it. <b>Lift</b> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(geometric)#Vector_components" title="Vector (geometric)">component</a> of this force that is perpendicular to the oncoming flow direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_is_Lift_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-What_is_Lift-1">[1]</a></sup> It contrasts with the <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">drag</a> force, which is the component of the force parallel to the flow direction. Lift conventionally acts in an upward direction in order to counter the force of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, but it can act in any direction at right angles to the flow.
</p><p>If the surrounding fluid is air, the force is called an <a href="/wiki/Aerodynamic_force" title="Aerodynamic force">aerodynamic force</a>. In water or any other liquid, it is called a <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">hydrodynamic force</a>.
</p><p>Dynamic lift is distinguished from other kinds of lift in fluids. <a href="/wiki/Aerostatics" title="Aerostatics">Aerostatic</a> lift or <a href="/wiki/Buoyancy" title="Buoyancy">buoyancy</a>, in which an internal fluid is lighter than the surrounding fluid, does not require movement and is used by balloons, blimps, dirigibles, boats, and submarines. <a href="/wiki/Planing_(boat)" title="Planing (boat)">Planing lift</a>, in which only the lower portion of the body is immersed in a liquid flow, is used by motorboats, surfboards, and water-skis.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Airfoil_lift_and_drag.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2484" data-file-width="4504" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Airfoil_lift_and_drag.jpg/300px-Airfoil_lift_and_drag.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Airfoil_lift_and_drag.jpg/450px-Airfoil_lift_and_drag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Airfoil_lift_and_drag.jpg/600px-Airfoil_lift_and_drag.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Airfoil_lift_and_drag.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lift is defined as the component of the total aerodynamic force perpendicular to the flow direction, and drag is the component parallel to the flow direction.</div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluid</a> flowing past the surface of a body exerts a <a href="/wiki/Surface_force" title="Surface force">force</a> on it. It makes no difference whether the fluid is flowing past a stationary body or the body is moving through a stationary volume of fluid. <b>Lift</b> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(geometric)#Vector_components" title="Vector (geometric)">component</a> of this force that is perpendicular to the oncoming flow direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_is_Lift_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-What_is_Lift-1">[1]</a></sup> Lift is always accompanied by a <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">drag</a> force, which is the component of the surface force parallel to the flow direction.
</p><p>Lift is most commonly associated with the <a href="/wiki/Wing" title="Wing">wings</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fixed-wing_aircraft" title="Fixed-wing aircraft">fixed-wing aircraft</a>, although it is more generally generated by many other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Streamlined" title="Streamlined">streamlined</a> bodies such as <a href="/wiki/Propeller" title="Propeller">propellers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kite_types" title="Kite types">kites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helicopter_rotor" title="Helicopter rotor">helicopter rotors</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wing_(automotive)" title="Wing (automotive)">racing car wings</a>, maritime <a href="/wiki/Sail" title="Sail">sails</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a> in air, and by <a href="/wiki/Sailboat" title="Sailboat">sailboat</a> <a href="/wiki/Keel" title="Keel">keels</a>, ship's <a href="/wiki/Rudder" title="Rudder">rudders</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrofoil" title="Hydrofoil">hydrofoils</a> in water. Lift is also exploited in the animal world, especially by <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bats</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, and even in the plant world by the seeds of certain trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the common meaning of the word "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/lift#English" title="wikt:lift">lift</a>" assumes that lift opposes weight, lift can be in any direction with respect to gravity, since it is defined with respect to the direction of flow rather than to the direction of gravity. When an aircraft is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cruise_(flight)" title="Cruise (flight)">cruising</a> in straight and level flight, most of the lift opposes gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> However, when an aircraft is <a href="/wiki/Climb_(aeronautics)" title="Climb (aeronautics)">climbing</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descent_(aircraft)" title="Descent (aircraft)">descending</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Banked_turn#Aviation" title="Banked turn">banking</a> in a turn the lift is tilted with respect to the vertical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Lift may also act as <a href="/wiki/Downforce" title="Downforce">downforce</a> in some <a href="/wiki/Aerobatics" title="Aerobatics">aerobatic manoeuvres</a>, or on the wing on a racing car. Lift may also be largely horizontal, for instance on a sailing ship.
</p><p>The lift discussed in this article is mainly in relation to airfoils, although marine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrofoils" title="Hydrofoils">hydrofoils</a> and propellers share the same physical principles and work in the same way, despite differences between air and water such as density, compressibility, and viscosity.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Simplified_physical_explanations_of_lift_on_an_airfoil">Simplified physical explanations of lift on an airfoil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Simplified physical explanations of lift on an airfoil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Airfoil_cross_section.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1626" data-file-width="2224" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Airfoil_cross_section.jpg/240px-Airfoil_cross_section.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Airfoil_cross_section.jpg/360px-Airfoil_cross_section.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Airfoil_cross_section.jpg/480px-Airfoil_cross_section.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Airfoil_cross_section.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cross-section of a wing defines an airfoil shape.</div></div></div>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Airfoil" title="Airfoil">airfoil</a> is a streamlined shape that is capable of generating significantly more lift than drag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> A flat plate can generate lift, but not as much as a streamlined airfoil, and with somewhat higher drag.
</p><p>There are several ways to explain how an airfoil generates lift. Some are more complicated or more physically rigorous than others; some have been shown to be incorrect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> For example, there are explanations based directly on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%E2%80%99s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton’s laws of motion">Newton’s laws of motion</a> and explanations based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bernoulli%E2%80%99s_principle" title="Bernoulli’s principle">Bernoulli’s principle</a>. Either can be used to explain lift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Flow_deflection_and_Newton.27s_laws"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Flow_deflection_and_Newton's_laws">Flow deflection and Newton's laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Flow deflection and Newton's laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AirfoilDeflectionLift_W3C.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/AirfoilDeflectionLift_W3C.svg/240px-AirfoilDeflectionLift_W3C.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/AirfoilDeflectionLift_W3C.svg/360px-AirfoilDeflectionLift_W3C.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/AirfoilDeflectionLift_W3C.svg/480px-AirfoilDeflectionLift_W3C.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AirfoilDeflectionLift_W3C.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>When an airfoil deflects air downwards, Newton's third law requires that the air must exert an equal upward reaction on the airfoil.</div></div></div>
<p>An airfoil generates lift by exerting a downward force on the air as it flows past. According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_third_law" title="Newton's third law">Newton's third law</a>, the air must exert an equal and opposite (upward) force on the airfoil, which is lift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_and_Eberhardt_2001_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson_and_Eberhardt_2001-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langewiesche_1944_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Langewiesche_1944-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The airflow changes direction as it passes the airfoil and follows a path that is curved downward. According to Newton's second law, this change in flow direction requires a downward force applied to the air by the airfoil. Then Newton's third law requires the air to exert an upward force on the airfoil; thus a reaction force, lift, is generated opposite to the directional change. In the case of an airplane wing, the wing exerts a downward force on the air and the air exerts an upward force on the wing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The downward turning of the flow is not produced solely by the lower surface of the airfoil, and the air flow above the airfoil accounts for much of the downward-turning action.
</p>
<h3><span id="Increased_flow_speed_and_Bernoulli.27s_principle"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Increased_flow_speed_and_Bernoulli's_principle">Increased flow speed and Bernoulli's principle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Increased flow speed and Bernoulli's principle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bernoulli%27s_principle" title="Bernoulli's principle">Bernoulli's principle</a> states that there is a direct mathematical relationship between the pressure of a fluid and the speed of that fluid, so if one knows the speed at all points within the airflow one can calculate the pressure, and vice versa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> For any airfoil generating lift, there must be a pressure imbalance, i.e. lower average air pressure on the top than on the bottom. Bernoulli's principle states that this pressure difference must be accompanied by a speed difference.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_of_mass">Conservation of mass</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Conservation of mass">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Streamlines_around_a_NACA_0012.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="164" data-file-width="302" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Streamlines_around_a_NACA_0012.svg/300px-Streamlines_around_a_NACA_0012.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Streamlines_around_a_NACA_0012.svg/450px-Streamlines_around_a_NACA_0012.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Streamlines_around_a_NACA_0012.svg/600px-Streamlines_around_a_NACA_0012.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Streamlines_around_a_NACA_0012.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Streamlines and streamtubes around an airfoil generating lift. Note the narrower streamtubes above and the wider streamtubes below.</div></div></div>
<p>Starting with the flow pattern observed in both theory and experiments, the increased flow speed over the upper surface can be explained in terms of streamtube pinching and <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_mass" title="Conservation of mass">conservation of mass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>For incompressible flow, the rate of volume flow (e.g. volume units per minute) must be constant within each streamtube since matter is not created or destroyed. If a streamtube becomes narrower, the flow speed must increase in the narrower region to maintain the constant flow rate, to satisfy the principle of <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_mass" title="Conservation of mass">conservation of mass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The upper streamtubes constrict as they flow up and around the airfoil. Conservation of mass says that the flow speed must increase as the stream tube area decreases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-24">[24]</a></sup> Similarly, the lower streamtubes expand and their flowrate slows.
</p><p>From Bernoulli's principle, the pressure on the upper surface where the flow is moving faster is lower than the pressure on the lower surface where it is moving slower. This pressure difference creates a net aerodynamic force, pointing upward.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Limitations_of_the_simplified_explanations">Limitations of the simplified explanations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Limitations of the simplified explanations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As explained below under <a href="#A_more_comprehensive_physical_explanation">a more comprehensive physical explanation</a>, producing a lift force requires maintaining pressure differences in both the vertical and horizontal directions, and thus requires both downward turning of the flow and changes in flow speed consistent with Bernoulli's principle. The simplified explanations given above are therefore incomplete because they define lift in terms of only one or the other. And depending on the details, they have other shortcomings as well.
</p><p>The explanation based on <a href="#Flow_deflection_and_Newton's_laws">flow deflection and Newton's laws</a> is correct but is incomplete. It does not explain how the airfoil can impart downward turning to a much deeper swath of the flow than it actually touches. Further, it doesn't explain how the pressure differences in the horizontal direction are sustained. That is, it leaves out the Bernoulli part of the interaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Explanations based on <a href="#Increased_flow_speed_and_Bernoulli's_principle">increased flow speed and Bernoulli's principle</a> first try to establish that there is higher flow speed over the upper surface, but they fail to explain correctly what causes the flow to speed up:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="#Conservation_of_mass">conservation of mass</a> explanation that relies on narrowing of the streamtubes over the upper surface does not explain why the streamtubes change size. To see why the air flows the way it does requires more sophisticated analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li>Sometimes a geometrical argument is offered to demonstrate why the streamtubes change size: it is asserted that the top "obstructs" or "constricts" the air more than the bottom, hence narrower streamtubes. For conventional wings that are flat on the bottom and curved on top this makes some intuitive sense. But it does not explain how flat plates, symmetric airfoils, sailboat sails, or conventional airfoils flying upside down can generate lift, and attempts to calculate lift based on the amount of constriction do not predict experimental results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li>A common version that relies on equal-transit-time is simply wrong, as explained below under <a href="#False_explanation_based_on_equal_transit-time">False explanation based on equal transit-time</a>.</li></ul>
<p>Bernoulli-only explanations imply that a speed difference arises from causes other than a pressure difference, and that the speed difference then leads to a pressure difference by Bernoulli’s principle. This implied one-way causation is a misconception. The real cause-and-effect relationship between pressure and velocity is reciprocal. Finally, Bernoulli-only explanations don't explain how the pressure differences in the vertical direction are sustained. That is, they leave out the downward-turning part of the interaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Alternative_explanations.2C_misconceptions.2C_and_controversies"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_explanations,_misconceptions,_and_controversies">Alternative explanations, misconceptions, and controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Alternative explanations, misconceptions, and controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many alternative explanations for the generation of lift by an airfoil have been put forward, most intended to explain the phenomenon of lift to a general audience. Although the explanations may share features in common with the explanations above, additional assumptions and simplifications may be introduced. Some explanations introduce assumptions which proved to be wrong, such as <i>equal transit-time</i>, and some used controversial terminology, such as "Coanda effect".
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="False_explanation_based_on_equal_transit-time">False explanation based on equal transit-time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: False explanation based on equal transit-time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:588px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Equal_transit-time_NASA_wrong1.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="264" data-file-width="586" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2d/Equal_transit-time_NASA_wrong1.gif" width="586"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Equal_transit-time_NASA_wrong1.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration of the incorrect equal transit-time explanation of airfoil lift.</div></div></div>
<p>Basic or popular sources often describe the "equal transit-time" theory of lift, which incorrectly assumes that the parcels of air that divide at the leading edge of an airfoil must rejoin at the trailing edge, forcing the air traveling along the longer upper surface to go faster. Bernoulli's principle is then cited to conclude that since the air moves slower along the bottom of the wing, the air pressure must be higher, pushing the wing up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, there is no physical principle that requires equal transit time and experimental results show that this assumption is false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> In fact, the air moving over the top of an airfoil generating lift moves <i>much</i> <i>faster</i> than the equal transit theory predicts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Further, the theory violates <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion#Newton's_3rd_Law" title="Newton's laws of motion">Newton's third law of motion</a>, since it describes a force on the wing with no opposite force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The assertion that the air must arrive simultaneously at the trailing edge is sometimes referred to as the "equal transit-time fallacy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Controversy_regarding_the_Coand.C4.83_effect"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy_regarding_the_Coandă_effect">Controversy regarding the Coandă effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Controversy regarding the Coandă effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coand%C4%83_effect" title="Coandă effect">Coandă effect</a></div>
<p>In its original sense, the <i>Coandă effect</i> refers to the tendency of a <a href="/wiki/Jet_(fluid)" title="Jet (fluid)">fluid jet</a> to stay attached to an adjacent surface that curves away from the flow, and the resultant <a href="/wiki/Entrainment_(hydrodynamics)" title="Entrainment (hydrodynamics)">entrainment</a> of ambient air into the flow. The effect is named for <a href="/wiki/Henri_Coand%C4%83" title="Henri Coandă">Henri Coandă</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romanian</a> aerodynamicist who exploited it in many of his patents.
</p><p>More broadly, some consider the effect to include the tendency of any fluid <a href="/wiki/Boundary_layer" title="Boundary layer">boundary layer</a> to adhere to a curved surface, not just the boundary layer accompanying a fluid jet. It is in this broader sense that the Coandă effect is used by some to explain why airflow remains attached to the top side of an airfoil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scotteberhart_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-scotteberhart-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jef_Raskin" title="Jef Raskin">Jef Raskin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raskin_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-raskin-50">[50]</a></sup> for example, describes a simple demonstration, using a straw to blow over the upper surface of a wing. The wing deflects upwards, thus demonstrating that the Coandă effect creates lift. This demonstration correctly demonstrates the Coandă effect as a fluid jet (the exhaust from a straw) adhering to a curved surface (the wing). However, the upper surface in this flow is a complicated, vortex-laden mixing layer, while on the lower surface the flow is <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/quiescence" title="wikt:quiescence">quiescent</a>. The physics of this demonstration are very different from that of the general flow over the wing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auerbach_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-auerbach-51">[51]</a></sup> The usage in this sense is encountered in some popular references on aerodynamics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scotteberhart_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-scotteberhart-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raskin_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-raskin-50">[50]</a></sup> This is a controversial use of the term "Coanda effect." The more established view in the aerodynamics field is that the Coandă effect is defined in the more limited sense above,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auerbach_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-auerbach-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and the flow following the upper surface simply reflects an absence of boundary-layer separation; thus it is not an example of the Coandă effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Basic_attributes_of_lift">Basic attributes of lift</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Basic attributes of lift">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Lift is a result of pressure differences and depends on angle of attack, airfoil shape, air density, and airspeed.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pressure_differences">Pressure differences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Pressure differences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">Pressure</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)#Normal_and_shear_stresses" title="Stress (mechanics)">normal force</a> per unit area exerted by the air on itself and on surfaces that it touches. The lift force is transmitted through the pressure, which acts perpendicular to the surface of the airfoil. Thus, the net force manifests itself as pressure differences. The direction of the net force implies that the average pressure on the upper surface of the airfoil is lower than the average pressure on the underside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>These pressure differences arise in conjunction with the curved airflow. When a fluid follows a curved path, there is a pressure <a href="/wiki/Gradient" title="Gradient">gradient</a> perpendicular to the flow direction with higher pressure on the outside of the curve and lower pressure on the inside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> This direct relationship between curved streamlines and pressure differences, sometimes called the <a href="/wiki/Euler_equations_(fluid_dynamics)#Streamline_curvature_theorem" title="Euler equations (fluid dynamics)">streamline curvature theorem</a>, was derived from Newton's second law by <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a> in 1754:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {\operatorname {d} p}{\operatorname {d} R}}=\rho {\frac {v^{2}}{R}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>p</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>R</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>ρ<!-- ρ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<msup>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mi>R</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {\operatorname {d} p}{\operatorname {d} R}}=\rho {\frac {v^{2}}{R}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\frac{\operatorname{d}p}{\operatorname{d}R}= \rho \frac{v^2}{R} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2b56f3817e2e33da9f68a1b6f2c3008a3ace9770" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:11.598ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>The left side of this equation represents the pressure difference perpendicular to the fluid flow. On the right hand side ρ is the density, v is the velocity, and R is the radius of curvature. This formula shows that higher velocities and tighter curvatures create larger pressure differentials and that for straight flow (R → ∞) the pressure difference is zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Angle_of_attack">Angle of attack</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Angle of attack">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Airfoil_angle_of_attack.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="246" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="92" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Airfoil_angle_of_attack.jpg/300px-Airfoil_angle_of_attack.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Airfoil_angle_of_attack.jpg/450px-Airfoil_angle_of_attack.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Airfoil_angle_of_attack.jpg/600px-Airfoil_angle_of_attack.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Airfoil_angle_of_attack.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Angle of attack of an airfoil</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_attack" title="Angle of attack">angle of attack</a> is the angle between the <a href="/wiki/Chord_(aeronautics)" title="Chord (aeronautics)">chord line</a> of an airfoil and the oncoming airflow. A symmetrical airfoil will generate zero lift at zero angle of attack. But as the angle of attack increases, the air is deflected through a larger angle and the vertical component of the airstream velocity increases, resulting in more lift. For small angles a symmetrical airfoil will generate a lift force roughly proportional to the angle of attack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the angle of attack increases, the lift reaches a maximum at some angle; increasing the angle of attack beyond this <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_attack#Critical_angle_of_attack" title="Angle of attack">critical angle of attack</a> causes the upper-surface flow to separate from the wing; there is less deflection downward so the airfoil generates less lift. The airfoil is said to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stall_(flight)" title="Stall (flight)">stalled</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Airfoil_shape">Airfoil shape</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Airfoil shape">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Airfoil_camber.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2024" data-file-width="4170" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Airfoil_camber.jpg/300px-Airfoil_camber.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Airfoil_camber.jpg/450px-Airfoil_camber.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Airfoil_camber.jpg/600px-Airfoil_camber.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Airfoil_camber.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An airfoil with camber compared to a symmetrical airfoil</div></div></div>
<p>The lift force depends on the shape of the airfoil, especially the amount of <a href="/wiki/Camber_(aerodynamics)" title="Camber (aerodynamics)">camber</a> (curvature such that the upper surface is more convex than the lower surface, as illustrated at right). Increasing the camber generally increases lift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cambered airfoils will generate lift at zero angle of attack. When the chord line is horizontal, the trailing edge has a downward direction and since the air follows the trailing edge it is deflected downward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> When a cambered airfoil is upside down, the angle of attack can be adjusted so that the lift force is upwards. This explains how a plane can fly upside down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flow_conditions">Flow conditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Flow conditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The ambient flow conditions which affect lift include the fluid density, viscosity and speed of flow. Density is affected by temperature, and by the medium's acoustic velocity - i.e. by compressibility effects.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_speed_and_density">Air speed and density</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Air speed and density">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lift is proportional to the density of the air and approximately proportional to the square of the flow speed. Lift also depends on the size of the wing, being generally proportional to the wing's area projected in the lift direction. In calculations it is convenient to quantify lift in terms of a <a href="/wiki/Lift_coefficient" title="Lift coefficient">lift coefficient</a> based on these factors.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boundary_layer_and_profile_drag">Boundary layer and profile drag</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Boundary layer and profile drag">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1915ca_abger_fluegel_(cropped_and_mirrored).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1855" data-file-width="2663" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/1915ca_abger_fluegel_%28cropped_and_mirrored%29.jpg/300px-1915ca_abger_fluegel_%28cropped_and_mirrored%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/1915ca_abger_fluegel_%28cropped_and_mirrored%29.jpg/450px-1915ca_abger_fluegel_%28cropped_and_mirrored%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/1915ca_abger_fluegel_%28cropped_and_mirrored%29.jpg/600px-1915ca_abger_fluegel_%28cropped_and_mirrored%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1915ca_abger_fluegel_(cropped_and_mirrored).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Airflow separating from a wing at a high angle of attack</div></div></div>
<p>No matter how smooth the surface of an airfoil seems, any surface is rough on the scale of air molecules. Air molecules flying into the surface bounce off the rough surface in random directions relative to their original velocities. The result is that when the air is viewed as a continuous material, it is seen to be unable to slide along the surface, and the air's velocity relative to the airfoil decreases to nearly zero at the surface (i.e., the air molecules "stick" to the surface instead of sliding along it), something known as the <a href="/wiki/No-slip_condition" title="No-slip condition">no-slip condition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Because the air at the surface has near-zero velocity but the air away from the surface is moving, there is a thin <a href="/wiki/Boundary_layer" title="Boundary layer">boundary layer</a> in which air close to the surface is subjected to a <a href="/wiki/Shear_force" title="Shear force">shearing</a> motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_1991,_Chapter_17_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson_1991,_Chapter_17-71">[71]</a></sup> The air's <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> resists the shearing, giving rise to a shear stress at the airfoil's surface called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skin_friction#Skin_friction" title="Skin friction">skin-friction drag</a>. Over most of the surface of most airfoils, the boundary layer is naturally turbulent, which increases skin-friction drag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_1991,_Chapter_17_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson_1991,_Chapter_17-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doenhoff_1958_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doenhoff_1958-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under usual flight conditions, the boundary layer remains attached to both the upper and lower surfaces all the way to the trailing edge, and its effect on the rest of the flow is modest. Compared to the predictions of <a href="/wiki/Inviscid_flow" title="Inviscid flow">inviscid flow</a> theory, in which there is no boundary layer, the attached boundary layer reduces the lift by a modest amount and modifies the pressure distribution somewhat, which results in a viscosity-related pressure drag over and above the skin-friction drag. The total of the skin-friction drag and the viscosity-related pressure drag is usually called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Profile_drag#Form_drag" title="Profile drag">profile drag</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doenhoff_1958_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Doenhoff_1958-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stalling">Stalling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Stalling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An airfoil's maximum lift at a given airspeed is limited by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boundary_layer_separation" title="Boundary layer separation">boundary-layer separation</a>. As the angle of attack is increased, a point is reached where the boundary layer can no longer remain attached to the upper surface. When the boundary layer separates, it leaves a region of recirculating flow above the upper surface, as illustrated in the flow-visualization photo at right. This is known as the <i>stall</i>, or <i>stalling</i>. At angles of attack above the stall, lift is significantly reduced, though it does not drop to zero. The maximum lift that can be achieved before stall, in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Lift_coefficient" title="Lift coefficient">lift coefficient</a>, is generally less than 1.5 for single-element airfoils and can be more than 3.0 for airfoils with high-lift slotted flaps and leading-edge devices deployed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bluff_bodies">Bluff bodies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Bluff bodies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Vortex_shedding" title="Vortex shedding">Vortex shedding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vortex-induced_vibration" title="Vortex-induced vibration">Vortex-induced vibration</a></div>
<p>The flow around <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bluff#Adjective" title="wikt:bluff">bluff</a> bodies – i.e. without a <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/streamline" title="wikt:streamline">streamlined</a> shape, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stall_(flight)" title="Stall (flight)">stalling</a> airfoils – may also generate lift, in addition to a strong drag force. This lift may be steady, or it may <a href="/wiki/Oscillation" title="Oscillation">oscillate</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Vortex_shedding" title="Vortex shedding">vortex shedding</a>. Interaction of the object's flexibility with the vortex shedding may enhance the effects of fluctuating lift and cause <a href="/wiki/Vortex-induced_vibration" title="Vortex-induced vibration">vortex-induced vibrations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williamson_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williamson-75">[75]</a></sup> For instance, the flow around a circular cylinder generates a <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n_vortex_street" title="Kármán vortex street">Kármán vortex street</a>: <a href="/wiki/Vortex" title="Vortex">vortices</a> being shed in an alternating fashion from the cylinder's sides. The oscillatory nature of the flow produces a fluctuating lift force on the cylinder, even though the net (mean) force is negligible. The lift force <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a> is characterised by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dimensionless" title="Dimensionless">dimensionless</a> <a href="/wiki/Strouhal_number" title="Strouhal number">Strouhal number</a>, which depends on the <a href="/wiki/Reynolds_number" title="Reynolds number">Reynolds number</a> of the flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>For a flexible structure, this oscillatory lift force may induce <a href="/wiki/Vortex-induced_vibration" title="Vortex-induced vibration">vortex-induced vibrations</a>. Under certain conditions – for instance <a href="/wiki/Resonance" title="Resonance">resonance</a> or strong spanwise <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Correlation" title="Correlation">correlation</a> of the lift force – the resulting motion of the structure due to the lift fluctuations may be strongly enhanced. Such vibrations may pose problems and threaten collapse in tall man-made structures like industrial <a href="/wiki/Chimney" title="Chimney">chimneys</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williamson_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williamson-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Magnus_effect" title="Magnus effect">Magnus effect</a>, a lift force is generated by a spinning cylinder in a freestream. Here the mechanical rotation acts on the boundary layer, causing it to separate at different locations on the two sides of the cylinder. The asymmetric separation changes the effective shape of the cylinder as far as the flow is concerned such that the cylinder acts like a lifting airfoil with circulation in the outer flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="A_more_comprehensive_physical_explanation">A more comprehensive physical explanation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: A more comprehensive physical explanation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As described above under "<a href="#Simplified_physical_explanations_of_lift_on_an_airfoil">Simplified physical explanations of lift on an airfoil</a>", there are two main popular explanations: one based on downward deflection of the flow (Newton's laws), and one based on pressure differences accompanied by changes in flow speed (Bernoulli's principle). Either of these, by itself, correctly identifies some aspects of the lifting flow but leaves other important aspects of the phenomenon unexplained. A more comprehensive explanation involves both downward deflection and pressure differences (including changes in flow speed associated with the pressure differences), and requires looking at the flow in more detail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lift_at_the_airfoil_surface">Lift at the airfoil surface</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Lift at the airfoil surface">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The airfoil shape and angle of attack work together so that the airfoil exerts a downward force on the air as it flows past. According to Newton's third law, the air must then exert an equal and opposite (upward) force on the airfoil, which is the lift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langewiesche_1944_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Langewiesche_1944-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The net force exerted by the air occurs as a pressure difference over the airfoil's surfaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milne-Thomson_1966,_Section_1.41_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Milne-Thomson_1966,_Section_1.41-80">[80]</a></sup> Pressure in a fluid is always positive in an absolute sense,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> so that pressure must always be thought of as pushing, and never as pulling. The pressure thus pushes inward on the airfoil everywhere on both the upper and lower surfaces.  The flowing air reacts to the presence of the wing by reducing the pressure on the wing's upper surface and increasing the pressure on the lower surface. The pressure on the lower surface pushes up harder than the reduced pressure on the upper surface pushes down, and the net result is upward lift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Milne-Thomson_1966,_Section_1.41_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Milne-Thomson_1966,_Section_1.41-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>The pressure difference which results in lift acts directly on the airfoil surfaces; however, understanding how the pressure difference is produced requires understanding what the flow does over a wider area.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_wider_flow_around_the_airfoil">The wider flow around the airfoil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: The wider flow around the airfoil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karman_trefftz.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="180" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/99/Karman_trefftz.gif" width="360"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption">Flow around an airfoil: the dots move with the flow. The black dots are on <a href="/wiki/Streamlines,_streaklines,_and_pathlines" title="Streamlines, streaklines, and pathlines">time slices</a>, which split into two – an upper and lower part – at the leading edge. A marked speed difference between the upper-and lower-surface streamlines is shown most clearly in the image animation, with the upper markers arriving at the trailing edge long before the lower ones. Colors of the dots indicate <a href="/wiki/Streamlines,_streaklines,_and_pathlines" title="Streamlines, streaklines, and pathlines">streamlines</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Airfoil_isobars.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3106" data-file-width="2095" decoding="async" height="445" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Airfoil_isobars.jpg/300px-Airfoil_isobars.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Airfoil_isobars.jpg/450px-Airfoil_isobars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Airfoil_isobars.jpg/600px-Airfoil_isobars.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Airfoil_isobars.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pressure distribution with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isobar_(meteorology)" title="Isobar (meteorology)">isobars</a> around a lifting airfoil. The plus sign indicates pressure higher than ambient, and the minus sign indicates pressure lower than ambient (NOT negative pressure in the absolute sense). The block arrows indicate the directions of net forces on fluid parcels in different parts of the flowfield.</div></div></div>
<p>An airfoil affects the speed and direction of the flow over a wide area, producing a pattern called a <i>velocity field</i>. When an airfoil produces lift, the flow ahead of the airfoil is deflected upward, the flow above and below the airfoil is deflected downward, and the flow behind the airfoil is deflected upward again, leaving the air far behind the airfoil in the same state as the oncoming flow far ahead. The flow above the upper surface is sped up, while the flow below the airfoil is slowed down. Together with the upward deflection of air in front and the downward deflection of the air immediately behind, this establishes a net circulatory component of the flow. The downward deflection and the changes in flow speed are pronounced and extend over a wide area, as can be seen in the flow animation on the right. These differences in the direction and speed of the flow are greatest close to the airfoil and decrease  gradually far above and below.  All of these features of the velocity field also appear in theoretical models for lifting flows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clancy_1975,_Section_4.5_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clancy_1975,_Section_4.5-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>The pressure is also affected over a wide area, in a pattern of non-uniform pressure called a <i>pressure field</i>. When an airfoil produces lift, there is a diffuse region of low pressure above the airfoil, and usually a diffuse region of high pressure below, as illustrated by the isobars (curves of constant pressure) in the drawing. The pressure difference that acts on the surface is just part of this pressure field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mutual_interaction_of_pressure_differences_and_changes_in_flow_velocity">Mutual interaction of pressure differences and changes in flow velocity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Mutual interaction of pressure differences and changes in flow velocity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The non-uniform pressure exerts forces on the air in the direction from higher pressure to lower pressure. The direction of the force is different at different locations around the airfoil, as indicated by the block arrows in the <i>pressure distribution with isobars</i> figure. Air above the airfoil is pushed toward the center of the low-pressure region, and air below the airfoil is pushed outward from the center of the high-pressure region.
</p><p>According to <i>Newton's second law</i>, a force causes air to accelerate in the direction of the force. Thus the vertical arrows in the <i>pressure distribution with isobars</i> figure indicate that air above and below the airfoil is accelerated, or turned downward, and that the non-uniform pressure is thus the cause of the downward deflection of the flow visible in the flow animation. To produce this downward turning, the airfoil must have a positive angle of attack or have its rear portion curved downward as on an airfoil with camber. Note that the downward turning of the flow over the upper surface is the result of the air being pushed downward by higher pressure above it than below it. Some explanations that refer to the "Coandă effect" suggest that viscosity plays a key role in the downward turning, but this is false. (see below under "<a href="#Controversy_regarding_the_Coandă_effect">Controversy regarding the Coandă effect</a>").
</p><p>The arrows ahead of the airfoil indicate that the flow ahead of the airfoil is deflected upward, and the arrows behind the airfoil indicate that the flow behind is deflected upward again, after being deflected downward over the airfoil.  These deflections are also visible in the flow animation.
</p><p>The arrows ahead of the airfoil and behind also indicate that air passing through the low-pressure region above the airfoil is sped up as it enters, and slowed back down as it leaves. Air passing through the high-pressure region below the airfoil sees the opposite - it is slowed down and then sped up. Thus the non-uniform pressure is also the cause of the changes in flow speed visible in the flow animation. The changes in flow speed are consistent with <i>Bernoulli's principle</i>, which states that in a steady flow without viscosity, lower pressure means higher speed, and higher pressure means lower speed.
</p><p>Thus changes in flow direction and speed are directly caused by the non-uniform pressure. But this cause-and-effect relationship is not just one-way; it works in both directions simultaneously. The air's motion is affected by the pressure differences, but the existence of the pressure differences depends on the air's motion. The relationship is thus a mutual, or reciprocal, interaction: Air flow changes speed or direction in response to pressure differences, and the pressure differences are sustained by the air's resistance to changing speed or direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> A pressure difference can exist only if something is there for it to push against.  In aerodynamic flow, the pressure difference pushes against the air's inertia, as the air is accelerated by the pressure difference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> This is why the air's mass is part of the calculation, and why lift depends on air density.
</p><p>Sustaining the pressure difference that exerts the lift force on the airfoil surfaces requires sustaining a pattern of non-uniform pressure in a wide area around the airfoil. This requires maintaining pressure differences in both the vertical and horizontal directions, and thus requires both downward turning of the flow and changes in flow speed according to Bernoulli's principle. The pressure differences and the changes in flow direction and speed sustain each other in a mutual interaction. The pressure differences follow naturally from Newton's second law and from the fact that flow along the surface follows the predominantly downward-sloping contours of the airfoil. And the fact that the air has mass is crucial to the interaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="How_simpler_explanations_fall_short">How simpler explanations fall short</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: How simpler explanations fall short">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Producing a lift force requires both downward turning of the flow and changes in flow speed consistent with Bernoulli's principle.  Each of the simplified explanations given above in <a href="#Simplified_physical_explanations_of_lift_on_an_airfoil">Simplified physical explanations of lift on an airfoil</a> falls short by trying to explain lift in terms of only one or the other, thus explaining only part of the phenomenon and leaving other parts unexplained. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantifying_lift">Quantifying lift</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Quantifying lift">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pressure_integration">Pressure integration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Pressure integration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When the pressure distribution on the airfoil surface is known, determining the total lift requires adding up the contributions to the pressure force from local elements of the surface, each with its own local value of pressure. The total lift is thus the <a href="/wiki/Integral" title="Integral">integral</a> of the pressure, in the direction perpendicular to the farfield flow, over the airfoil surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle L=\oint p\mathbf {n} \cdot \mathbf {k} \;\mathrm {d} S,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>L</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-OP MJX-fixedlimits">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-VCENTER">
<mstyle mathsize="2.07em">

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mrow>

<mi>p</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi>S</mi>

</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle L=\oint p\mathbf {n} \cdot \mathbf {k} \;\mathrm {d} S,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8341c1d6fecb290597c41881fda2a1ab4f0304c3" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:18.606ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span>
</p><p>where:
</p>
<ul><li><b>S</b> is the projected (planform) area of the airfoil, measured normal to the mean airflow;</li>
<li><b>n</b> is the normal unit vector pointing into the wing;</li>
<li><b>k</b> is the vertical unit vector, normal to the freestream direction.</li></ul>
<p>The above lift equation neglects the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skin_friction" title="Skin friction">skin friction</a> forces, which are small compared to the pressure forces.
</p><p>By using the streamwise vector <b>i</b> parallel to the freestream in place of <b>k</b> in the integral, we obtain an expression for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressure_drag" title="Pressure drag">pressure drag</a> <i>D<sub>p</sub></i> (which includes the pressure portion of the profile drag and, if the wing is three-dimensional, the <a href="/wiki/Lift-induced_drag" title="Lift-induced drag">induced drag</a>). If we use the spanwise vector <b>j</b>, we obtain the side force <i>Y</i>.
</p>

<p>The validity of this integration generally requires the airfoil shape to be a closed curve that is piecewise smooth.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lift_coefficient">Lift coefficient</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Lift coefficient">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lift_coefficient" title="Lift coefficient">lift coefficient</a></div>
<p>Lift depends on the size of the wing, being approximately proportional to the wing area. It is often convenient to quantify the lift of a given airfoil by its <i>lift coefficient</i> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C_{L}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>L</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C_{L}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="C_{L}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6bd8f93b1f511b05ced33ab7fcfceab8072cdb01" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.013ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, which defines its overall lift in terms of a unit area of the wing.
</p><p>If the value of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C_{L}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>

</p>
