<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematical_theories_of_lift">Mathematical theories of lift</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Mathematical theories of lift">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Mathematical theories of lift are based on continuum fluid mechanics, assuming that air flows as a continuous fluid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Lift is generated in accordance with the fundamental principles of physics, the most relevant being the following three principles:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_momentum" title="Conservation of momentum">Conservation of momentum</a>, which is a consequence of <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton's laws of motion">Newton's laws of motion</a>, especially Newton's second law which relates the net <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a> on an element of air to its rate of <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> change,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Continuity_equation#Fluid_dynamics" title="Continuity equation">Conservation of mass</a>, including the assumption that the airfoil's surface is impermeable for the air flowing around, and</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_energy" title="Conservation of energy">Conservation of energy</a>, which says that energy is neither created nor destroyed.</li></ul>
<p>Because an airfoil affects the flow in a wide area around it, the conservation laws of mechanics are embodied in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partial_differential_equations" title="Partial differential equations">partial-differential equations</a> combined with a set of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boundary_condition" title="Boundary condition">boundary condition</a> requirements which the flow has to satisfy at the airfoil surface and far away from the airfoil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>To predict lift requires solving the equations for a particular airfoil shape and flow condition, which generally requires calculations that are so voluminous that they are practical only on a computer, through the methods of <a href="/wiki/Computational_fluid_dynamics" title="Computational fluid dynamics">computational fluid dynamics</a> (CFD). Determining the net aerodynamic force from a CFD solution requires "adding up" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Integration_(mathematics)" title="Integration (mathematics)">integrating</a>) the forces due to pressure and shear determined by the CFD over every surface element of the airfoil as described under "<a href="#Pressure_integration">pressure integration</a>".
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Navier-Stokes_equations" title="Navier-Stokes equations">Navier-Stokes equations</a> (NS) provide the potentially most accurate theory of lift, but in practice, capturing the effects of turbulence in the boundary layer on the airfoil surface requires sacrificing some accuracy, and requires use of the <a href="/wiki/Reynolds-averaged_Navier%E2%80%93Stokes_equations" title="Reynolds-averaged Navier–Stokes equations">Reynolds-averaged Navier-Stokes equations</a> (RANS). Simpler but less accurate theories have also been developed.
</p>
<h3><span id="Navier-Stokes_.28NS.29_equations"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Navier-Stokes_(NS)_equations">Navier-Stokes (NS) equations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Navier-Stokes (NS) equations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>These equations represent conservation of mass, Newton's second law (conservation of momentum), conservation of energy, the <a href="/wiki/Viscosity#Newtonian_and_non-Newtonian_fluids" title="Viscosity">Newtonian law for the action of viscosity</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fourier_heat_conduction_equation#Fourier.27s_law" title="Fourier heat conduction equation">Fourier heat conduction law</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Equation_of_state" title="Equation of state">equation of state</a> relating density, temperature, and pressure, and formulas for the viscosity and thermal conductivity of the fluid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>In principle, the NS equations, combined with boundary conditions of no through-flow and <a href="/wiki/No-slip_condition" title="No-slip condition">no slip</a> at the airfoil surface, could be used to predict lift in any situation in ordinary atmospheric flight with high accuracy. However, airflows in practical situations always involve turbulence in the boundary layer next to the airfoil surface, at least over the aft portion of the airfoil. Predicting lift by solving the NS equations in their raw form would require the calculations to resolve the details of the turbulence, down to the smallest eddy. This is not yet possible, even on the most powerful current computer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spalart_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spalart-99">[99]</a></sup> So in principle the NS equations provide a complete and very accurate theory of lift, but practical prediction of lift requires that the effects of turbulence be modeled in the RANS equations rather than computed directly.
</p>
<h3><span id="Reynolds-Averaged_Navier-Stokes_.28RANS.29_equations"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reynolds-Averaged_Navier-Stokes_(RANS)_equations">Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) equations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) equations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>These are the NS equations with the turbulence motions averaged over time, and the effects of the turbulence on the time-averaged flow represented by <a href="/wiki/Turbulence_modeling" title="Turbulence modeling">turbulence modeling</a> (an additional set of equations based on a combination of <a href="/wiki/Dimensional_analysis" title="Dimensional analysis">dimensional analysis</a> and empirical information on how turbulence affects a boundary layer in a time-averaged average sense).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> A RANS solution consists of the time-averaged velocity vector, pressure, density, and temperature defined at a dense grid of points surrounding the airfoil.
</p><p>The amount of computation required is a minuscule fraction (billionths)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spalart_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spalart-99">[99]</a></sup> of what would be required to resolve all of the turbulence motions in a raw NS calculation, and with large computers available it is now practical to carry out RANS calculations for complete airplanes in three dimensions. Because turbulence models are not perfect, the accuracy of RANS calculations is imperfect, but it is adequate for practical aircraft design. Lift predicted by RANS is usually within a few percent of the actual lift.
</p>
<h3><span id="Inviscid-flow_equations_.28Euler_or_potential.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Inviscid-flow_equations_(Euler_or_potential)">Inviscid-flow equations (Euler or potential)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Inviscid-flow equations (Euler or potential)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Euler_equations_(fluid_dynamics)" title="Euler equations (fluid dynamics)">Euler equations</a> are the NS equations without the viscosity, heat conduction, and turbulence effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> As with a RANS solution, an Euler solution consists of the velocity vector, pressure, density, and temperature defined at a dense grid of points surrounding the airfoil. While the Euler equations are simpler than the NS equations, they do not lend themselves to exact analytic solutions.
</p><p>Further simplification is available through <a href="/wiki/Potential_flow" title="Potential flow">potential flow</a> theory, which reduces the number of unknowns to be determined, and makes analytic solutions possible in some cases, as described below.
</p><p>Either Euler or potential-flow calculations predict the pressure distribution on the airfoil surfaces roughly correctly for angles of attack below stall, where they might miss the total lift by as much as 10-20%. At angles of attack above stall, inviscid calculations do not predict that stall has happened, and as a result they grossly overestimate the lift.
</p><p>In potential-flow theory, the flow is assumed to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irrotational" title="Irrotational">irrotational</a>, i.e. that small fluid parcels have no net rate of rotation. Mathematically, this is expressed by the statement that the <a href="/wiki/Curl_(mathematics)" title="Curl (mathematics)">curl</a> of the velocity vector field is everywhere equal to zero. Irrotational flows have the convenient property that the velocity can be expressed as the <a href="/wiki/Gradient" title="Gradient">gradient</a> of a scalar function called a <a href="/wiki/Potential" title="Potential">potential</a>. A flow represented in this way is called <a href="/wiki/Potential_flow" title="Potential flow">potential flow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>In potential-flow theory, the flow is assumed to be incompressible. Incompressible potential-flow theory has the advantage that the equation (<a href="/wiki/Laplace%27s_equation" title="Laplace's equation">Laplace's equation</a>) to be solved for the potential is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear#Physics" title="Linear">linear</a>, which allows solutions to be constructed by <a href="/wiki/Superposition_principle" title="Superposition principle">superposition</a> of other known solutions. The incompressible-potential-flow equation can also be solved by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conformal_mapping" title="Conformal mapping">conformal mapping</a>, a method based on the theory of functions of a complex variable. In the early 20th century, before computers were available, conformal mapping was used to generate solutions to the incompressible potential-flow equation for a class of idealized airfoil shapes, providing some of the first practical theoretical predictions of the pressure distribution on a lifting airfoil.
</p><p>A solution of the potential equation directly determines only the velocity field. The pressure field is deduced from the velocity field through Bernoulli's equation.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Airfoil_Kutta_condition.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1821" data-file-width="3750" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Airfoil_Kutta_condition.jpg/400px-Airfoil_Kutta_condition.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Airfoil_Kutta_condition.jpg/600px-Airfoil_Kutta_condition.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Airfoil_Kutta_condition.jpg/800px-Airfoil_Kutta_condition.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Airfoil_Kutta_condition.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of a non-lifting flow pattern around an airfoil and a lifting flow pattern consistent with the Kutta condition, in which the flow leaves the trailing edge smoothly</div></div></div>
<p>Applying potential-flow theory to a lifting flow requires special treatment and an additional assumption. The problem arises because lift on an airfoil in inviscid flow requires <a href="/wiki/Circulation_(fluid_dynamics)" title="Circulation (fluid dynamics)">circulation</a> in the flow around the airfoil (See "<a href="#Circulation_and_the_Kutta-Joukowski_theorem">Circulation and the Kutta-Joukowski theorem</a>" below), but a single potential function that is continuous throughout the domain around the airfoil cannot represent a flow with nonzero circulation. The solution to this problem is to introduce a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Branch_cut" title="Branch cut">branch cut</a>, a curve or line from some point on the airfoil surface out to infinite distance, and to allow a jump in the value of the potential across the cut. The jump in the potential imposes circulation in the flow equal to the potential jump and thus allows nonzero circulation to be represented. However, the potential jump is a free parameter that is not determined by the potential equation or the other boundary conditions, and the solution is thus indeterminate. A potential-flow solution exists for any value of the circulation and any value of the lift. One way to resolve this indeterminacy is to impose the <a href="/wiki/Kutta_condition" title="Kutta condition">Kutta condition</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> which is that, of all the possible solutions, the physically reasonable solution is the one in which the flow leaves the trailing edge smoothly. The streamline sketches illustrate one flow pattern with zero lift, in which the flow goes around the trailing edge and leaves the upper surface ahead of the trailing edge, and another flow pattern with positive lift, in which the flow leaves smoothly at the trailing edge in accordance with the Kutta condition.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linearized_potential_flow">Linearized potential flow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Linearized potential flow">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This is potential-flow theory with the further assumptions that the airfoil is very thin and the angle of attack is small.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> The linearized theory predicts the general character of the airfoil pressure distribution and how it is influenced by airfoil shape and angle of attack, but is not accurate enough for design work. For a 2D airfoil, such calculations can be done in a fraction of a second in a spreadsheet on a PC.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Circulation_and_the_Kutta-Joukowski_theorem">Circulation and the Kutta-Joukowski theorem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Circulation and the Kutta-Joukowski theorem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Circulation-around-aerofoil.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="159" data-file-width="279" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Circulation-around-aerofoil.svg/300px-Circulation-around-aerofoil.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Circulation-around-aerofoil.svg/450px-Circulation-around-aerofoil.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Circulation-around-aerofoil.svg/600px-Circulation-around-aerofoil.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Circulation-around-aerofoil.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Circulation component of the flow around an airfoil</div></div></div>
<p>When an airfoil generates lift, several components of the overall velocity field contribute to a net circulation of air around it: the upward flow ahead of the airfoil, the accelerated flow above, the decelerated flow below, and the downward flow behind.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Circulation_(fluid_dynamics)" title="Circulation (fluid dynamics)">circulation</a> can be understood as the total amount of "spinning" (or <a href="/wiki/Vorticity" title="Vorticity">vorticity</a>) of an inviscid fluid around the airfoil.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Kutta%E2%80%93Joukowski_theorem" title="Kutta–Joukowski theorem">Kutta–Joukowski theorem</a> relates the lift per unit width of span of a two-dimensional airfoil to this circulation component of the flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clancy_1975,_Section_4.5_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clancy_1975,_Section_4.5-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> It is a key element in an explanation of lift that follows the development of the flow around an airfoil as the airfoil starts its motion from rest and a <a href="/wiki/Starting_vortex" title="Starting vortex">starting vortex</a> is formed and left behind, leading to the formation of circulation around the airfoil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Lift is then inferred from the Kutta-Joukowski theorem. This explanation is largely mathematical, and its general progression is based on logical inference, not physical cause-and-effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Kutta-Joukowski model does not predict how much circulation or lift a two-dimensional airfoil will produce. Calculating the lift per unit span using Kutta-Joukowski requires a known value for the circulation. In particular, if the <a href="/wiki/Kutta_condition" title="Kutta condition">Kutta condition</a> is met, in which the rear stagnation point moves to the airfoil trailing edge and attaches there for the duration of flight, the lift can be calculated theorically through the conformal mapping method.
</p><p>The lift generated by a conventional airfoil is dictated by both its design and the flight conditions, such as forward velocity, angle of attack and air density. Lift can be increased by artificially increasing the circulation, for example by boundary-layer blowing or the use of <a href="/wiki/Blown_flap" title="Blown flap">blown flaps</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Flettner_rotor" title="Flettner rotor">Flettner rotor</a> the entire airfoil is circular and spins about a spanwise axis to create the circulation.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Three-dimensional_flow">Three-dimensional flow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Three-dimensional flow">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wing_isobars.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1150" data-file-width="3220" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Wing_isobars.jpg/300px-Wing_isobars.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Wing_isobars.jpg/450px-Wing_isobars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Wing_isobars.jpg/600px-Wing_isobars.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wing_isobars.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cross-section of an airplane wing-body combination showing the isobars of the three-dimensional lifting flow</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wing_velocity_vectors.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1182" data-file-width="3200" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Wing_velocity_vectors.jpg/300px-Wing_velocity_vectors.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Wing_velocity_vectors.jpg/450px-Wing_velocity_vectors.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Wing_velocity_vectors.jpg/600px-Wing_velocity_vectors.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wing_velocity_vectors.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cross-section of an airplane wing-body combination showing velocity vectors of the three-dimensional lifting flow</div></div></div>
<p>The flow around a three-dimensional wing involves significant additional issues, especially relating to the wing tips. For a wing of low aspect ratio, such as a typical <a href="/wiki/Delta_wing" title="Delta wing">delta wing</a>, two-dimensional theories may provide a poor model and three-dimensional flow effects can dominate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Even for wings of high aspect ratio, the three-dimensional effects associated with finite span can affect the whole span, not just close to the tips.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wing_tips_and_spanwise_distribution">Wing tips and spanwise distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Wing tips and spanwise distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The vertical pressure gradient at the wing tips causes air to flow sideways, out from under the wing then up and back over the upper surface. This reduces the pressure gradient at the wing tip, therefore also reducing lift. The lift tends to decrease in the spanwise direction from root to tip, and the pressure distributions around the airfoil sections change accordingly in the spanwise direction. Pressure distributions in planes perpendicular to the flight direction tend to look like the illustration at right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> This spanwise-varying pressure distribution is sustained by a mutual interaction with the velocity field. Flow below the wing is accelerated outboard, flow outboard of the tips is accelerated upward, and flow above the wing is accelerated inboard, which results in the flow pattern illustrated at right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is more downward turning of the flow than there would be in a two-dimensional flow with the same airfoil shape and sectional lift, and a higher sectional angle of attack is required to achieve the same lift compared to a two-dimensional flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The wing is effectively flying in a downdraft of its own making, as if the freestream flow were tilted downward, with the result that the total aerodynamic force vector is tilted backward slightly compared to what it would be in two dimensions. The additional backward component of the force vector is called <a href="/wiki/Lift-induced_drag" title="Lift-induced drag">lift-induced drag</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tip_vortex_rollup.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="551" data-file-width="743" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Tip_vortex_rollup.png/300px-Tip_vortex_rollup.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Tip_vortex_rollup.png/450px-Tip_vortex_rollup.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Tip_vortex_rollup.png/600px-Tip_vortex_rollup.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tip_vortex_rollup.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Euler computation of a tip vortex rolling up from the trailed vorticity sheet</div></div></div>
<p>The difference in the spanwise component of velocity above and below the wing (between being in the inboard direction above and in the outboard direction below) persists at the trailing edge and into the wake downstream. After the flow leaves the trailing edge, this difference in velocity takes place across a relatively thin shear layer called a vortex sheet.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Horseshoe_vortex_system">Horseshoe vortex system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Horseshoe vortex system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wing_horseshoe_vortex.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="3332" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Wing_horseshoe_vortex.jpg/300px-Wing_horseshoe_vortex.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Wing_horseshoe_vortex.jpg/450px-Wing_horseshoe_vortex.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Wing_horseshoe_vortex.jpg/600px-Wing_horseshoe_vortex.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wing_horseshoe_vortex.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Planview of a wing showing the horseshoe vortex system</div></div></div>
<p>The wingtip flow leaving the wing creates a tip vortex. As the main vortex sheet passes downstream from the trailing edge, it rolls up at its outer edges, merging with the tip vortices. The combination of the <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_vortices" title="Wingtip vortices">wingtip vortices</a> and the vortex sheets feeding them is called the vortex wake.
</p><p>In addition to the vorticity in the trailing vortex wake there is vorticity in the wing's boundary layer, called 'bound vorticity', which connects the trailing sheets from the two sides of the wing into a vortex system in the general form of a horseshoe. The horseshoe form of the vortex system was recognized by the British aeronautical pioneer Lanchester in 1907.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Given the distribution of bound vorticity and the vorticity in the wake, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biot-Savart_law" title="Biot-Savart law">Biot-Savart law</a> (a vector-calculus relation) can be used to calculate the velocity perturbation anywhere in the field, caused by the lift on the wing. Approximate theories for the lift distribution and lift-induced drag of three-dimensional wings are based on such analysis applied to the wing's horseshoe vortex system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> In these theories, the bound vorticity is usually idealized and assumed to reside at the camber surface inside the wing.
</p><p>Because the velocity is deduced from the vorticity in such theories, some authors describe the situation to imply that the vorticity is the cause of the velocity perturbations, using terms such as "the velocity induced by the vortex", for example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> But attributing mechanical cause-and-effect between  the vorticity and the velocity in this way is not consistent with the physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The velocity perturbations in the flow around a wing are in fact produced by the pressure field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Manifestations_of_lift_in_the_farfield">Manifestations of lift in the farfield</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Manifestations of lift in the farfield">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Integrated_force.2Fmomentum_balance_in_lifting_flows"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Integrated_force/momentum_balance_in_lifting_flows">Integrated force/momentum balance in lifting flows</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Integrated force/momentum balance in lifting flows">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Airfoil_control_volumes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5824" data-file-width="5554" decoding="async" height="419" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Airfoil_control_volumes.jpg/400px-Airfoil_control_volumes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Airfoil_control_volumes.jpg/600px-Airfoil_control_volumes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Airfoil_control_volumes.jpg/800px-Airfoil_control_volumes.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Airfoil_control_volumes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Control volumes of different shapes that have been used in analyzing the momentum balance in the 2D flow around a lifting airfoil. The airfoil is assumed to exert a downward force -L' per unit span on the air, and the proportions in which that force is manifested as momentum fluxes and pressure differences at the outer boundary are indicated for each different shape of control volume.</div></div></div>
<p>The flow around a lifting airfoil must satisfy Newton's second law regarding conservation of momentum, both locally at every point in the flow field, and in an integrated sense over any extended region of the flow. For an extended region, Newton's second law takes the form of the <i>momentum theorem for a control volume</i>, where a <a href="/wiki/Control_volume" title="Control volume">control volume</a> can be any region of the flow chosen for analysis. The momentum theorem states that the integrated force exerted at the boundaries of the control volume (a surface integral), is equal to the integrated time rate of change (<a href="/wiki/Material_derivative" title="Material derivative">material derivative</a>) of the momentum of fluid parcels passing through the interior of the control volume. For a steady flow, this can be expressed in the form of the net surface integral of the flux of momentum through the boundary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>The lifting flow around a 2D airfoil is usually analyzed in a control volume that completely surrounds the airfoil, so that the inner boundary of the control volume is the airfoil surface, where the downward force per unit span <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle -L'}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<msup>
<mi>L</mi>
<mo>′</mo>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle -L'}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="-L'" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3931e98988127dcc8b46d2ff7772ddecd324a762" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:4.076ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> is exerted on the fluid by the airfoil. The outer boundary is usually either a large circle or a large rectangle. At this outer boundary distant from the airfoil, the velocity and pressure are well represented by the velocity and pressure associated with a uniform flow plus a vortex, and viscous stress is negligible, so that the only force that must be integrated over the outer boundary is the pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lissaman_1996_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lissaman_1996-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durand_1932_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Durand_1932-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Batchelor_1967_p._407_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Batchelor_1967_p._407-131">[131]</a></sup> The free-stream velocity is usually assumed to be horizontal, with lift vertically upward, so that the vertical momentum is the component of interest.
</p><p>For the free-air case (no ground plane), the force <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle -L'}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<msup>
<mi>L</mi>
<mo>′</mo>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle -L'}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="-L'" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3931e98988127dcc8b46d2ff7772ddecd324a762" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:4.076ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> exerted by the airfoil on the fluid is manifested partly as momentum fluxes and partly as pressure differences at the outer boundary, in proportions that depend on the shape of the outer boundary, as shown in the diagram at right. For a flat horizontal rectangle that is much longer than it is tall, the fluxes of vertical momentum through the front and back are negligible, and the lift is accounted for entirely by the integrated pressure differences on the top and bottom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lissaman_1996_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lissaman_1996-129">[129]</a></sup> For a square or circle, the momentum fluxes and pressure differences account for half the lift each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lissaman_1996_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lissaman_1996-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durand_1932_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-Durand_1932-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Batchelor_1967_p._407_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-Batchelor_1967_p._407-131">[131]</a></sup> For a vertical rectangle that is much taller than it is wide, the unbalanced pressure forces on the top and bottom are negligible, and lift is accounted for entirely by momentum fluxes, with a flux of upward momentum that enters the control volume through the front accounting for half the lift, and a flux of downward momentum that exits the control volume through the back accounting for the other half.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lissaman_1996_129-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lissaman_1996-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>The results of all of the control-volume analyses described above are consistent with the Kutta-Joukowski theorem described above. Both the tall rectangle and circle control volumes have been used in derivations of the theorem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durand_1932_130-2"><a href="#cite_note-Durand_1932-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Batchelor_1967_p._407_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-Batchelor_1967_p._407-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lift_reacted_by_overpressure_on_the_ground_under_an_airplane">Lift reacted by overpressure on the ground under an airplane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lift_(force)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Lift reacted by overpressure on the ground under an airplane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pressure_footprint_isometric_b.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2504" data-file-width="4052" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pressure_footprint_isometric_b.jpg/400px-Pressure_footprint_isometric_b.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pressure_footprint_isometric_b.jpg/600px-Pressure_footprint_isometric_b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pressure_footprint_isometric_b.jpg/800px-Pressure_footprint_isometric_b.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pressure_footprint_isometric_b.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of the distribution of higher-than-ambient pressure on the ground under an airplane in flight</div></div></div>
<p>An airfoil produces a pressure field in the surrounding air, as explained under "<a href="#The_wider_flow_around_the_airfoil">The wider flow around the airfoil</a>" above. The pressure differences associated with this field die off gradually, becoming very small at large distances, but never disappearing altogether. Below the airplane, the pressure field persists as a positive pressure disturbance that reaches the ground, forming a pattern of slightly-higher-than-ambient pressure on the ground, as shown on the right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Although the pressure differences are very small far below the airplane, they are spread over a wide area and add up to a substantial force. For steady, level flight, the integrated force due to the pressure differences is equal to the total aerodynamic lift of the airplane and to the airplane's weight. According to Newton's third law, this pressure force exerted on the ground by the air is matched by an equal-and-opposite upward force exerted on the air by the ground, which offsets all of the downward force exerted on the air by the airplane. The net force due to the lift, acting on the atmosphere as a whole, is therefore zero, and thus there is no integrated accumulation of vertical momentum in the atmosphere, as was noted by Lanchester early in the development of modern aerodynamics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p><br/>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The lift on the body is simple...it's the reaction of the solid body to the turning of a moving fluid...Now why does the fluid turn the way that it does? That's where the complexity enters in because we are dealing with a fluid. ...The cause for the flow turning is the simultaneous conservation of mass, momentum (both linear and angular), and energy by the fluid. And it's confusing for a fluid because the mass can move and redistribute itself (unlike a solid), but can only do so in ways that conserve momentum (mass times velocity) and energy (mass times velocity squared)... A change in velocity in one direction can cause a change in velocity in a perpendicular direction in a fluid, which doesn't occur in solid mechanics... So exactly describing how the flow turns is a complex problem; too complex for most people to visualize. So we make up simplified "models". And when we simplify, we leave something out. So the model is flawed. Most of the arguments about lift generation come down to people finding the flaws in the various models, and so the arguments are usually very legitimate." Tom Benson of NASA's Glenn Research Center in an interview with AlphaTrainer.Com <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.alphatrainer.com/pages/corner.htm" rel="nofollow">"Archived copy - Tom Benson Interview"</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120427005906/http://www.alphatrainer.com/pages/corner.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on April 27, 2012<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">26 July</span> 2012</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Archived+copy+-+Tom+Benson+Interview&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.alphatrainer.com%2Fpages%2Fcorner.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Langewiesche_1944-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Langewiesche_1944_15-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Langewiesche_1944_15-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Langewiesche (1944)</span>

<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"When air flows over and under an airfoil inclined at a small angle to its direction, the air is turned from its course. Now, when a body is moving in a uniform speed in a straight line, it requires force to alter either its direction or speed. Therefore, the sails exert a force on the wind and, since action and reaction are equal and opposite, the wind exerts a force on the sails." In: <cite class="citation" id="CITEREFMorwood">Morwood, John, <i>Sailing Aerodynamics</i>, Adlard Coles Limited, p. 17</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Sailing+Aerodynamics&amp;rft.pages=17&amp;rft.pub=Adlard+Coles+Limited&amp;rft.aulast=Morwood&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Lift is a force generated by turning a moving fluid... If the body is shaped, moved, or inclined in such a way as to produce a net deflection or turning of the flow, the local velocity is changed in magnitude, direction, or both. Changing the velocity creates a net force on the body." <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.grc.nasa.gov/WWW/K-12/airplane/right2.html" rel="nofollow">"Lift from Flow Turning"</a>. NASA Glenn Research Center. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110705131653/http://www.grc.nasa.gov/WWW/K-12/airplane/right2.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on July 5, 2011<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">July 7,</span> 2009</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Lift+from+Flow+Turning&amp;rft.pub=NASA+Glenn+Research+Center&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.grc.nasa.gov%2FWWW%2FK-12%2Fairplane%2Fright2.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Essentially, due to the presence of the wing (its shape and inclination to the incoming flow, the so-called angle of attack), the flow is given a downward deflection. It is Newton’s third law at work here, with the flow then exerting a reaction force on the wing in an upward direction, thus generating lift." Vassilis Spathopoulos - Flight Physics for Beginners: Simple Examples of Applying Newton’s Laws <i>The Physics Teacher</i> Vol. 49, September 2011 p. 373 <a class="external autonumber" href="https://archive.is/20130618032326/http://tpt.aapt.org/resource/1/phteah/v49/i6/p373_s1" rel="nofollow">[2]</a> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The main fact of all heavier-than-air flight is this: <i>the wing keeps the airplane up by pushing the air down.</i>" In: Langewiesche - <i>Stick and Rudder</i>, p. 6</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Birds and aircraft fly because they are constantly pushing air downwards:  L = Δp/Δt  where L= lift force, and Δp/Δt is the rate at which downward momentum is imparted to the airflow." <i>Flight without Bernoulli</i> Chris Waltham <i>THE PHYSICS TEACHER</i> Vol. 36, Nov. 1998 <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.df.uba.ar/users/sgil/physics_paper_doc/papers_phys/fluids/fly_no_bernoulli.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Archived copy"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110928200519/http://www.df.uba.ar/users/sgil/physics_paper_doc/papers_phys/fluids/fly_no_bernoulli.pdf" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span> from the original on September 28, 2011<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">4 August</span> 2011</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Archived+copy&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.df.uba.ar%2Fusers%2Fsgil%2Fphysics_paper_doc%2Fpapers_phys%2Ffluids%2Ffly_no_bernoulli.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><span class="cs1-maint citation-comment">CS1 maint: Archived copy as title (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_Archived_copy_as_title" title="Category:CS1 maint: Archived copy as title">link</a>)</span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Clancy, L. J.; <i>Aerodynamics</i>, Pitman 1975, p. 76: "This lift force has its reaction in the downward momentum which is imparted to the air as it flows over the wing. Thus the lift of the wing is equal to the rate of transport of downward momentum of this air."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"...if the air is to produce an upward force on the wing, the wing must produce a downward force on the air. Because under these circumstances air cannot sustain a force, it is deflected, or accelerated, downward. Newton's second law gives us the means for quantifying the lift force: F<sub>lift</sub> = m∆v/∆t = ∆(mv)/∆t. The lift force is equal to the time rate of change of momentum of the air." <cite class="citation journal">Smith, Norman F. (1972). "Bernoulli and Newton in Fluid Mechanics". <i>The Physics Teacher</i>: 451. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1972PhTea..10..451S" rel="nofollow">1972PhTea..10..451S</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1119%2F1.2352317" rel="nofollow">10.1119/1.2352317</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Physics+Teacher&amp;rft.atitle=Bernoulli+and+Newton+in+Fluid+Mechanics&amp;rft.pages=451&amp;rft.date=1972&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1119%2F1.2352317&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1972PhTea..10..451S&amp;rft.aulast=Smith&amp;rft.aufirst=Norman+F.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Smith, Norman F. (1973). <li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"There is no way to predict, from Bernoulli's equation alone, what the pattern of streamlines will be for a particular wing." <li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The generation of lift may be explained by starting from the shape of streamtubes above and below an airfoil. <li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"There is nothing wrong with the Bernoulli principle, or with the statement that the air goes faster over the top of the wing. But, as the above discussion suggests, our understanding is not complete with this explanation. The problem is that we are missing a vital piece when we apply Bernoulli’s principle. We can calculate the pressures around the wing if we know the speed of the air over and under the wing, but how do we determine the speed?" <i>How Airplanes Fly: A Physical Description of Lift</i> David Anderson and Scott Eberhardt <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.allstar.fiu.edu/aero/airflylvl3.htm" rel="nofollow">"Archived copy"</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160126200755/http://www.allstar.fiu.edu/aero/airflylvl3.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on January 26, 2016<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">26 January</span> 2016</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Archived+copy&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.allstar.fiu.edu%2Faero%2Fairflylvl3.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><span class="cs1-maint citation-comment">CS1 maint: Archived copy as title (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_Archived_copy_as_title" title="Category:CS1 maint: Archived copy as title">link</a>)</span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The problem with the 'Venturi' theory is that it attempts to provide us with the velocity based on an incorrect assumption (the constriction of the flow produces the velocity field). We can calculate a velocity based on this assumption, and use Bernoulli's equation to compute the pressure, and perform the pressure-area calculation and the answer we get does not agree with the lift that we measure for a given airfoil." NASA Glenn Research Center <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.grc.nasa.gov/WWW/K-12/airplane/wrong3.html" rel="nofollow">"Archived copy"</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120717222459/http://www.grc.nasa.gov/WWW/k-12/airplane/wrong3.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on July 17, 2012<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"A concept...uses a symmetrical convergent-divergent channel, like a longitudinal section of a Venturi tube, as the starting point . . when such a device is put in a flow, the static pressure in the tube decreases. When the upper half of the tube is removed, a geometry resembling the airfoil is left, and suction is still maintained on top of it. Of course, this explanation is flawed too, because the geometry change affects the whole flowfield and there is no physics involved in the description." <li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"This answers the apparent mystery of how a symmetric airfoil can produce lift. ... Eastlake <i>An Aerodynamicist’s View of Lift, Bernoulli, and Newton</i> <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.df.uba.ar/users/sgil/physics_paper_doc/papers_phys/fluids/Bernoulli_Newton_lift.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Archived copy"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090411055333/http://www.df.uba.ar/users/sgil/physics_paper_doc/papers_phys/fluids/Bernoulli_Newton_lift.pdf" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span> from the original on April 11, 2009<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"This classic explanation is based on the difference of streaming velocities caused by the airfoil. There remains, however, a question: How does the airfoil cause the difference in streaming velocities? Some books don't give any answer, while others just stress the picture of the streamlines, saying the airfoil reduces the separations of the streamlines at the upper side. They do not say how the airfoil manages to do this. Thus this is not a sufficient answer." Klaus Weltner <i>Bernoulli's Law and Aerodynamic Lifting Force</i> The Physics Teacher February 1990 p. 84. <a class="external autonumber" href="http://scitation.aip.org/getpdf/servlet/GetPDFServlet?filetype=pdf&amp;id=PHTEAH000028000002000084000001&amp;idtype=cvips&amp;prog=normal" rel="nofollow">[3]</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title=" Dead link since January 2018">permanent dead link</span></a></i>]</span></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> McLean 2012, Section 7.3.3.12 </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The airfoil of the airplane wing, according to the textbook explanation that is more or less standard in the United States, has a special shape with more curvature on top than on the bottom; consequently, the air must travel farther over the top surface than over the bottom surface. Because the air must make the trip over the top and bottom surfaces in the same elapsed time ..., the velocity over the top surface will be greater than over the bottom. According to Bernoulli's theorem, this velocity difference produces a pressure difference which is lift." <i>Bernoulli and Newton in Fluid Mechanics</i> Norman F. Smith <i>The Physics Teacher</i> November 1972 Volume 10, Issue 8, p. 451 <a class="external autonumber" href="http://scitation.aip.org/getpdf/servlet/GetPDFServlet?filetype=pdf&amp;id=PHTEAH000010000008000451000001&amp;idtype=cvips&amp;doi=10.1119/1.2352317&amp;prog=normal" rel="nofollow">[4]</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title=" Dead link since January 2018">permanent dead link</span></a></i>]</span></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Unfortunately, this explanation [fails] on three counts. First, an airfoil need not have more curvature on its top than on its bottom. Airplanes can and do fly with perfectly symmetrical airfoils; that is with airfoils that have the <i>same</i> curvature top and bottom. Second, even if a humped-up (cambered) shape is used, the claim that the air must traverse the curved top surface in the same time as it does the flat bottom surface...is fictional. We can quote no physical law that tells us this. Third—and this is the most serious—the common textbook explanation, and the diagrams that accompany it, describe a force on the wing with no net disturbance to the airstream. This constitutes a violation of Newton's third law." <i>Bernoulli and Newton in Fluid Mechanics</i> Norman F. Smith <i>The Physics Teacher</i> November 1972 Volume 10, Issue 8, p. 451 <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120317075304/http://tpt.aapt.org/resource/1/phteah/v10/i8" rel="nofollow">"Archived copy"</a>. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://tpt.aapt.org/resource/1/phteah/v10/i8" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on March 17, 2012<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">4 August</span> 2011</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Archived+copy&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Ftpt.aapt.org%2Fresource%2F1%2Fphteah%2Fv10%2Fi8&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><span class="cs1-maint citation-comment">CS1 maint: Archived copy as title (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_Archived_copy_as_title" title="Category:CS1 maint: Archived copy as title">link</a>)</span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<cite class="citation" id="CITEREFAnderson2001">Anderson, David (2001), <i>Understanding Flight</i>, New York: McGraw-Hill, p. 15, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-07-136377-7" title="Special:BookSources/0-07-136377-7">0-07-136377-7</a>, <q>The first thing that is wrong is that the principle of equal transit times is not true for a wing with lift.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Understanding+Flight&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pages=15&amp;rft.pub=McGraw-Hill&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft.isbn=0-07-136377-7&amp;rft.aulast=Anderson&amp;rft.aufirst=David&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Anderson, John (2005). <i>Introduction to Flight</i>. This is simply not true</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Introduction+to+Flight&amp;rft.place=Boston&amp;rft.pages=355&amp;rft.pub=McGraw-Hill+Higher+Education&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.isbn=0072825693&amp;rft.aulast=Anderson&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"...do you remember hearing that troubling business about the particles moving over the curved top surface having to go faster than the particles that went underneath, because they have a longer path to travel but must still get there at the same time? This is simply not true. It does not happen." Charles N. Eastlake <i>An Aerodynamicist’s View of Lift, Bernoulli, and Newton</i> <i>THE PHYSICS TEACHER</i> Vol. 40, March 2002 <a class="external text" href="http://www.df.uba.ar/users/sgil/physics_paper_doc/papers_phys/fluids/Bernoulli_Newton_lift.pdf" rel="nofollow">PDF</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090411055333/http://www.df.uba.ar/users/sgil/physics_paper_doc/papers_phys/fluids/Bernoulli_Newton_lift.pdf" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> April 11, 2009, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The actual velocity over the top of an airfoil is much faster than that predicted by the "Longer Path" theory and particles moving over the top arrive at the trailing edge before particles moving under the airfoil." <cite class="citation web">Glenn Research Center (March 15, 2006). <a class="external text" href="http://www.grc.nasa.gov/WWW/K-12/airplane/wrong1.html" rel="nofollow">"Incorrect Lift Theory"</a>. NASA. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140427084226/http://www.grc.nasa.gov/WWW/K-12/airplane/wrong1.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on April 27, 2014<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">August 12,</span> 2010</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Incorrect+Lift+Theory&amp;rft.pub=NASA&amp;rft.date=2006-03-15&amp;rft.au=Glenn+Research+Center&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.grc.nasa.gov%2FWWW%2FK-12%2Fairplane%2Fwrong1.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"...the air is described as producing a force on the object <i>without the object having any opposite effect on the air</i>. Such a condition, we should quickly recognize, embodies an <i>action</i> without a <i>reaction</i>, which is, according to Newton’s Third Law, impossible." Norman F. Smith <i>Bernoulli, Newton, and Dynamic Lift Part I</i> School Science and Mathematics, 73, 3, March 1973 <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://eric.ed.gov/?id=EJ075197" rel="nofollow">"Archived copy"</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20150119214246/http://eric.ed.gov/?id=EJ075197" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on January 19, 2015<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">19 January</span> 2015</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Archived+copy&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Feric.ed.gov%2F%3Fid%3DEJ075197&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><span class="cs1-maint citation-comment">CS1 maint: Archived copy as title (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_Archived_copy_as_title" title="Category:CS1 maint: Archived copy as title">link</a>)</span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A false explanation for lift has been put forward in mainstream books, and even in scientific exhibitions. Known as the "equal transit-time" explanation, it states that the parcels of air which are divided by an airfoil must rejoin again; because of the greater curvature (and hence longer path) of the upper surface of an aerofoil, the air going over the top must go faster in order to 'catch up' with the air flowing around the bottom. Therefore, because of its higher speed the pressure of the air above the airfoil must be lower. Despite the fact that this 'explanation' is probably the most common of all, it is false. It has recently been dubbed the "Equal transit-time fallacy".<cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.aviationexplorer.com/fixed_wing_aircraft.htm" rel="nofollow">"Fixed-wing aircraft facts and how aircraft fly"</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090603043832/http://aviationexplorer.com/fixed_wing_aircraft.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on June 3, 2009<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">July 7,</span> 2009</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Fixed-wing+aircraft+facts+and+how+aircraft+fly&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.aviationexplorer.com%2Ffixed_wing_aircraft.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">...it leaves the impression that Professor Bernoulli is somehow to blame for the "equal transit time" fallacy... <cite class="citation web">John S. Denker (1999). <a class="external text" href="http://www.av8n.com/fly/lift.htm" rel="nofollow">"Critique of "How Airplanes Fly<span class="cs1-kern-right">"</span>"</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20091120184040/http://www.av8n.com/fly/lift.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on November 20, 2009<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">July 7,</span> 2009</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Critique+of+%22How+Airplanes+Fly%22&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rft.au=John+S.+Denker&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.av8n.com%2Ffly%2Flift.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The fallacy of equal transit time can be deduced from consideration of a flat plate, which will indeed produce lift, as anyone who has handled a sheet of plywood in the wind can testify. <cite class="citation web">Gale M. Craig. <a class="external text" href="http://regenpress.com/" rel="nofollow">"Physical principles of winged flight"</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090802062641/http://regenpress.com/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on August 2, 2009<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">July 7,</span> 2009</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Physical+principles+of+winged+flight&amp;rft.au=Gale+M.+Craig&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fregenpress.com%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Fallacy 1: Air takes the same time to move across the top of an aerofoil as across the bottom. <cite class="citation" id="CITEREFPeter_Eastwell2007">Peter Eastwell (2007), <a class="external text" href="http://www.scienceeducationreview.com/open_access/eastwell-bernoulli.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Bernoulli? <li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"There is a popular fallacy called the equal transit-time fallacy that claims the two halves rejoin at the trailing edge of the aerofoil." <li id="cite_note-auerbach-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-auerbach_51-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-auerbach_51-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFAuerbach2000">Auerbach, David (2000), "Why Aircraft Fly", <i>Eur. <li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFWhite2002">White, Frank M. (2002), <i>Fluid Mechanics</i> (5th ed.), McGraw Hill</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Fluid+Mechanics&amp;rft.edition=5th&amp;rft.pub=McGraw+Hill&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.aulast=White&amp;rft.aufirst=Frank+M.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A uniform pressure surrounding a body does not create a net force. (See <a href="/wiki/Buoyancy" title="Buoyancy">buoyancy</a>). Therefore pressure differences are needed to exert a force on a body immersed in a fluid. For example, see: <cite class="citation" id="CITEREFBatchelor1967"><a href="/wiki/George_Batchelor" title="George Batchelor">Batchelor, G.K.</a> (1967), <i>An Introduction to Fluid Dynamics</i>, Cambridge University Press, pp. 14–15, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-521-66396-2" title="Special:BookSources/0-521-66396-2">0-521-66396-2</a></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=An+Introduction+to+Fluid+Dynamics&amp;rft.pages=14-15&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1967&amp;rft.isbn=0-521-66396-2&amp;rft.aulast=Batchelor&amp;rft.aufirst=G.K.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"<i>...if a streamline is curved, there must be a pressure gradient across the streamline...</i>"<cite class="citation" id="CITEREFBabinsky2003">Babinsky, Holger (November 2003), <a class="external text" href="http://www.iop.org/EJ/article/0031-9120/38/6/001/pe3_6_001.pdf" rel="nofollow">"How do wings work?"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>, <i>Physics Education</i>, <b>38</b>: 497, <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2003PhyEd..38..497B" rel="nofollow">2003PhyEd..38..497B</a>, <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1088%2F0031-9120%2F38%2F6%2F001" rel="nofollow">10.1088/0031-9120/38/6/001</a></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Physics+Education&amp;rft.atitle=How+do+wings+work%3F&amp;rft.volume=38&amp;rft.pages=497&amp;rft.date=2003-11&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1088%2F0031-9120%2F38%2F6%2F001&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2003PhyEd..38..497B&amp;rft.aulast=Babinsky&amp;rft.aufirst=Holger&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.iop.org%2FEJ%2Farticle%2F0031-9120%2F38%2F6%2F001%2Fpe3_6_001.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thus a distribution of the pressure is created which is given in Euler's equation. The physical reason is the aerofoil which forces the streamline to follow its curved surface. The low pressure at the upper side of the aerofoil is a consequence of the curved surface." <i>A comparison of explanations of the aerodynamic lifting force</i> Klaus Weltner Am. J. Phys. Vol.55 No.January 1, 1987 p. 53 <a class="external autonumber" href="http://aapt.scitation.org/doi/pdf/10.1119/1.14960" rel="nofollow">[5]</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"You can argue that the main lift comes from the fact that the wing is angled slightly upward so that air striking the underside of the wing is forced downward. The Newton's 3rd law reaction force upward on the wing provides the lift. Increasing the angle of attack can increase the lift, but it also increases drag so that you have to provide more thrust with the aircraft engines" <i>Hyperphysics</i> Georgia State University Dept. of Physics and Astronomy <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://hyperphysics.phy-astr.gsu.edu/hbase/fluids/angatt.html" rel="nofollow">"Archived copy"</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20121014185450/http://hyperphysics.phy-astr.gsu.edu/hbase/fluids/angatt.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on October 14, 2012<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">26 July</span> 2012</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Archived+copy&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fhyperphysics.phy-astr.gsu.edu%2Fhbase%2Ffluids%2Fangatt.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALift+%28force%29"></span><span class="cs1-maint citation-comment">CS1 maint: Archived copy as title (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_Archived_copy_as_title" title="Category:CS1 maint: Archived copy as title">link</a>)</span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"If we enlarge the angle of attack we enlarge the deflection of the airstream by the airfoil. This results in the enlargement of the vertical component of the velocity of the airstream... we may expect that the lifting force depends linearly on the angle of attack. This dependency is in complete agreement with the results of experiments..." Klaus Weltner <i>A comparison of explanations of the aerodynamic lifting force</i> Am. J. Phys. 55(1), January 1987 p. 52</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The decrease[d lift] of angles exceeding 25° is plausible. For large angles of attack we get turbulence and thus less deflection downward." Klaus Weltner <i>A comparison of explanations of the aerodynamic lifting force</i> <i>Am. J. Phys.</i> 55(1), January 1987 p. 52</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Abbott, and von Doenhoff (1958), Section 4.2</span>

<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"With an angle of attack of 0°, we can explain why we already have a lifting force. The air stream behind the aerofoil follows the trailing edge. The trailing edge already has a downward direction, if the chord to the middle line of the profile is horizontal." Klaus Weltner <i>A comparison of explanations of the aerodynamic lifting force</i> <i>Am. J. Phys.</i> 55(1), January 1987 p. 52</span>

<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"...the important thing about an aerofoil . . is not so much that its upper surface is humped and its lower surface is nearly flat, but simply that it moves through the air at an angle.
